B00000JMOF|5.0|Mary Shelley's Promethean tale follows the mad genius Dr. Henry Frankenstein as he attempts to create new life from dead tissue. His experiments are a success, but the being that rises from his slab is an abomination of science. Frankenstein is unable to contain his own creation, and the hulking brute is unleashed upon the unsuspecting village below.<br /><br />Although it was not the first time Shelley's novel had been adapted for the screen, James Whales will always be remembered as the man who gave life to the one true FRANKENSTEIN. The 1931 version would set the standard for every Gothic Horror film to follow, with its incredible sets, creeping atmosphere, and impeccable performances. Colin Clive shows a ferocious energy and an irresponsible enthusiasm as he declares \\"It's alive!\\" as the egomaniacal Dr. Frankenstein. The iconic look of the creature would be attributed to the great Jack Pierce, who would go on to design other such notable villains as The Mummy and The Wolf Man.<br /><br />But who is the mystery man behind the monster that is only identified by a question mark in the opening credits? Although Boris Karloff had starred in countless pictures prior to making FRANKENSTEIN, it would be this role that would jettison him into the limelight. Karloff would go on to star in dozens of Horror films in the decades to follow, but none of his performances (save for his reprisal of the character in BRIDE OF FRANKENSTEIN) would ever reach the same level of notoriety and critical acclaim. While his towering form combined with his menacing snarls made The Monster both fearsome and frightening, it is the tender moments like the death of Maria that best demonstrate Karloff's range and form as an actor. Few, if any, have even come close to creating such a terrifying and tragic character through their performance.<br /><br />FRANKENSTEIN is the greatest of any of Universal's classic monster movies, and yet James Whales would somehow manage to outdo himself four years later in the film's stunning sequel.<br /><br />-Carl Manes<br />I Like Horror Moviesng sequel.     -Carl Manes  I Like Horror Movies
B002PQ7JQK|5.0|I reviewed individual episodes of The Original Series episodes during the initial DVD release which began in 1999. So, the comments here focus on the remastered blu-ray edition.<br /><br />It goes without saying that the picture has never looked better. Colors pop off the screen, scratches have been removed, and grain, while noticeable, has been reduced to a bare minimum. Seeing the new remastering alters one's perceptions of the series. I had never noticed the tear streaming down the crippled Captain Pike's cheek as Spock reveals his plan in \\"The Menagerie.\\" Unfortunately, with the improved picture, flaws in the original production are also more noticeable: shadows from boom microphones are detectable, zippers can be clearly seen in many of the uniforms, and a certain hairpiece has never been more obvious. Still, the picture shows the obvious care taken with the lighting and composition - very different from today's flatly-lit, smash & grab style of television filming.<br /><br />Of course, the primary focus of this new release is the new CGI visual effects. Trek had a number of effects heavy episodes, including \\"The Doomsday Machine\\" and \\"The Tholian Web,\\" and they benefit the most from the new effects. Most of the new space shots are very convincing, remaining in the original spirit of the series. Only a few of the shots are major deviations from the originals, such as when the shuttle is seen exiting the Enterprise from the outside. Restraint seems to be the watchword here, and I would have liked to have seen a little bit more done, such as the replacement of some very dated view screen graphics in \\"The Changeling,\\" and correction to faulty composite shots, such as in \\"This Side of Paradise.\\"  But the enhanced background plate in \\"By Any Other Name\\" is simply stunning, and the expanded castle front seen in \\"Catspaw\\" (an otherwise weak episode) is subtly done.  Toggling between the CGI and original effects, I was struck by how much the old effects distracted me from the story - more so than when there was no CGI option.  I've already been spoiled. Ree's father Jessup has gone missing, but he put his property up for bond.  Meaning, if Ree doesn't find her father within a week--either that, or proof that he is dead--she looses the house and the land.  Unfortunately, her father's extended family seems inclined to let sleeping dogs lie, to the extent that Ree finds herself in genuine danger. A lot of the cast aren't even professional actors, which lends the film an extra air of authenticity (that, and the fact that it was shot on location).  WINTER'S BONE is, without a doubt, a small production; and while that can sometimes ruin a movie, here it helps.  The script is good though not wholly memorable; the real grit here lies in the actors' performances, without which this film would've been a mediocre idea and best forgotten.  As is, it's nominated for 4 Academy Awards, and goes down as one of the best films of 2010 (a year in which quite a few good movies were made).

I really did not know how or why our financial system collapsed the way that it did, nor am I sure I have a good handle on it now.  However, I do feel that this book opened my eyes a great deal into the way the bond market behaves, and the financial markets in general work and how corrupt the system really is.  Listening to a great deal of politics, one finds that each side blames the other and fingers are pointed all over the political map, just not at one's own party.  But this book did not enter into the political arena at all.  Instead, it showed how the financial institutions took what the political system gave them and twisted it to their own ends in order to make the most money possible and damn the consequences.  It was very eye opening.    Some of the characters in thestory were hilarious - from Eisman the \\"sincerely rude\\" individual to Dr. Burry who was essentially an isolationist, and all the idiots at Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, etc. who got gamed by their own system. I highly recommed this book.

0060555661|5.0|That quote sums up the Intelligent Investor and it's value based investing approach and has stuck in my mind since. This book, and it's updates by Jason Zweig, was excellently crafted and provided a fascinating history of the markets from the 19th to 21st century. For those that are looking to the book to be the gospel for investing, disuade yourself of that notion right away; even Warren Buffet who idolised Graham moved away (to a certain degree) from his strict investment approach of focusing on cheap stocks (relative to the underlying value of the company). The value of the book for me was realising that the volatility of the market wasn't confined to the events I knew; the prominent ones in the past, the 1929 & 1987 incidents and the more recent ones, the 2000 crash (after which I started working in fund management) and finally the 2007/2008 collapse. Reading Grahams book and the Zweig's updates you realise these boom bust cycles have been going on, with more or less the same frequency as recent times, since the markets first opened. New get rich approaches to investing in the stock market get dusted off and recycled, new more complex ones come along. In the short term no one can can consistently predict what the market will do let alone an indiviual stock, but in the long run through value analysis and the experiences in this book you can set yourself up with a solid foundation for successful investing.

0375507256|4.0|Cloud Atlas, so misleadingly referred to as a \\"novel,\\" is actually a cycle of short stories that follows the reincarnations of one soul on its journey through human history.  Each of the stories can be read by itself, but like any short story cycle, the work takes on new dimensions of meaning when the stories are read alongside one another.  Mitchell's gimmick, if it can be called that, interrupts each story at a cliffhanger; after five cliffhangers, and one story told without interruption, CA finishes out with a rapid-fire succession of denouements and ends where the story began, circa 1850.<br /><br />I would recommend CA, but hesitantly.  For those looking for a book resting somewhere between thoughtful literature and thoughtless pulp, Cloud Atlas is a good choice.  The characters are interesting and the plots are eccentric and fun, which makes CA an overall engaging read.  Mitchell's control of different styles is also quite admirable, and is exemplified (in my opinion) by the epistolary mode of Adam Ewing and the dialectal, post-apocalyptic romp of Zachry.  Each character sounds like themselves, and none of the characters rehashes any others.<br /><br />On the other hand, for those looking for a book that will challenge them intellectually or morally, or will introduce them to characters so near to flesh and blood that we forget their literary origins, look elsewhere.  The characters in CA were never deep or complex enough for my tastes and never emerged from the stories as real people.  Frobisher comes closest with his intriguing mix of aristocratic insouciance and volatile brashness, and his unquenchable passion for music.  No wonder Mitchell grants him such a meta-fictionally important role.  However, he never takes on the different dimensions one might expect a character of his potential to develop; while multifaceted, he lacks the light necessary to sparkle.<br /><br />Similarly, Mitchell seems to lack the maturity and wisdom to write a book of that particular sublime caliber for which some of us are always searching.  If you're willing to aim below sublime, however, then give this book a chance.  It's good, exciting fiction and definitely several cuts above most of the garbage circulating in print.or which some of us are always searching. B003VYAVGA|5.0|I LOVE this album.      I've never been a fan of Take That...traditional boyband stuff was just never my thing -     HOWEVER, I did fall in with once-former-now-returned Robbie Williams' solo work some ten years ago, when he began with his guitar-rooted, Oasis-emulating phase (loved them as a teenager) and he is probably the reason I came around to liking straight-up pop music at all (as opposed/in addition to popular & classic rock bands and indie).  He's interesting, he's evolved, very flawed and philosophical about it - and LOVES to talk about this with lyrics that are poetry (occasionally somewhat bad poetry, lol - but if you love him, it's enjoyable regardless) - and his best vocal moments are very raw & emotionally genuine. He appreciates a lot of pop genres - past , present & future - and plays around with them all, and has been incredibly successful with that approach pretty much everywhere but in the US.      ANYWAY - this album marks the return of Robbie to the fold of Take That - the band he quit/was fired from 15 years ago due to his substance abuse problems, forcing the band itself to break up shortly thereafter.  Robbie of course went on to become a huge solo star - and the band ultimately reformed without him around 2006 and made some great records, evolved from their old cheesy stuff, but still pretty much bland adult contemporary (in my opinion) - though of high quality.  Remarkable was that this band - 90% a dance act that got their start in gay clubs and wooed teen girls across the globe - made the leap from being typical manufactured dance/harmony-bots and began to take control of their own creative destiny and become a true band, more than a decade after they'd disbanded.    Robbie's return has brought further legitimacy to their control over their work, and there's a lot of his flavor on the record. He's always had an ability to deal with complex ideas in a simple, catchy, mass-consumable way (which shows more intelligence, not less, for not being esoteric, in my opinion). It's why he's a huge pop star.     Thanks to Rob (and to the chagrin of some TT fans, probably), this is not an album that panders to a swooning audience, but showcases the points-of-view & experiences of its makers.  And they are a bunch of British guys with working class roots, aged 37-42, contemplating aging, social anxiety, mature relationships, their collective pop past and the fate of the weird world we all now live in. Many songs have a lot of Robbie's trademark paranoia, grit & darkness lodged within, but the songs are still polished into fine pop confections - many dancey & beat-driven with a couple of bigger ballads. The harmonies they excel at persist, and you can tell it's not truly the Robbie Show - everyone bought in to the 'Progress' concept, contributed, and is having a great time with it.  It's a fun pop record, which works on both shallow & deeper levels.    After a great flow of tracks from \\"The Flood\\" to \\"Underground Machine\\", 'Progress' hits a bit of a wall with \\"What Do You Want From Me\\" - Mark Owen's song, which could've used more refinement from its slightly too raw & personal state. It's a bit of stumbling block to get past, with the remainder of the album's tracks - except the sappy glory of \\"Eight Letters\\" - notably lacking Robbie's songwriting vigor.  But at that point, if you've fallen for the redemptive story of the reformation of Take That, you overlook weakness and are interested to hear the stripped down POVs Gary, Mark, Howard & Jason have to contribute, too.    It's a really good album if you like strong pop albums.  It's a GREAT album if you're a Robbie fan, and/or are willing to dive into the overall Take That story behind it all.

030726999X|4.0|[[ASIN:030726999X The Girl Who Kicked the Hornet's Nest]]<br /><br />Picking up directly after the end of the previous volume and concluding the cliffhanger that ended it, The Girl Who Kicked The Hornet's Nest begins extracting shreds of justice for Lisbeth Salander as the story sprints towards the millennium trilogy's finale.<br /><br />As with the other two volumes, The Girl With The Dragon Tattoo, and The Girl Who Played With Fire, Larsson wastes little time with metaphorical flourishes or other narrative embellishments. The writing is straightforward and well-paced, and a lot of thorough description puts the reader in the mind of the characters and at the scene of the action.<br /><br />But first, Salander, the subject of a nationwide manhunt, has to be cleared of charges that she murdered three people. With his usual eye for exacting detail, Larsson sets about clearing Salander by describing all of the charges against her, which by necessity updates the reader on the story so far.<br /><br />You have to marvel at how much work Larsson has put into each of these three volumes, which displays an extraordinary amount of energy, research, and typing. However, devising a twin sister for Salander, who is barely mentioned in the three novels, and is never seen, is an unnecessary contrivance for a writer who is so adept at creating memorable main characters, and a plethora of ingenious supporting cast members.<br /><br />Larsson spends an entire short chapter describing the background and activities of the secret service operative who originally helped to send the young Salander to the psych ward, and now wants to prevent that plan from gaining public scrutiny. The result of this description is a narrative shockwave that pushes the story into a higher gear.<br /><br />In several divergences that reminds the reader that the story is also about journalists and journalism, Larsson shows former Millennium Magazine editor Erika Berger's first day at work at her new job as publisher of one of the country's large daily newspapers, describing the personal clashes and power struggles that she encounters. Woven into Berger's new duties is an excellent commentary on the media frenzy surrounding Salander's erroneous murder charges, which Berger helps a young reporter to understand, and shows him how to determine whether a source is being truthful.<br /><br />In another journalistic aside, a young Millennium Magazine reporter describes his meticulous investigative handiwork in uncovering fraud in the Swedish construction industry that has dire implications for Berger's new CEO.<br />We also witness the stalking incident that sent Berger to the emergency room. All of this detail is tightly woven into the fast-paced narrative, and the reader feels no need to skip any of it. Indeed, it enriches the narrative.<br /><br />The action moves to the courtroom as Bloomkvist convinces his sister, Annika Giannini, a women's rights attorney, to handle Salander's defense on the numerous charges she faces, even though the state prosecutor has dropped the three murder charges against her.<br /><br />As the prosecution mounts what appears to be an ironclad case against Salander, her attorney only offers minimal comments and objections until presenting a vigorous and ingenious defense, aided by a cadre of people who want to make sure Salander is not committed to an asylum again. As is his custom in this series, Larsson uses an immense amount of detail about the legal and criminal malfeasance that not only frees Salander, but topples the cadre of secret service operatives that helped to send the teenaged Salander to a mental institution.<br /><br />Once freed, Salander heads to Gibraltar to relax and the story begins to lag as she binges on alcohol and initiates sexual liaisons with strangers. The narrative tension only resumes when Salander returns to Sweden and confronts her half-brother, the murderous Ronald Niedermann, who tried to kill her in The Girl WhoPlayed With Fire.<br /><br />They engage in a mano-a-mano denouement that brings the series to a startling conclusion, and reunites Salander and Mikael Bloomkvist in what could likely be a prelude to another adventure. If only the Larsson family, and Stieg Larsson's longtime partner, Eva Gabrielsson, could agree on how and when to publish it.the series to a startling conclusion, and reunites Salander and Mikael Bloomkvist in what could likely be a prelude to another adventure. B004G600A4|5.0|Background:<br /><br />Tangled has its origins from the German fairy tale Rapunzel by the Brothers Grimm.<br />It was first published in 1812 as part of a collection of children's tales. It is believed that this story was an adaptation from a fairy tale called Persinette by Charlotte-Rose de Caumont de La Force which was originally published in 1698. It is a story that has been beaten to a pulp, and is best know for the line: \\"Rapunzel, Rapunzel, let down your hair\\".<br /><br />Even further back, this story may seem to be a modified feminized version of the Biblical story of Samson as related to in the book of Judges chapter 13. Here because Samson was a Nazarite a razor was never allowed to touch his hair.<br /><br />Marketing and Sales:<br /><br />This film was originally titled and marketed as Rapunzel, however, at the last minute the name was changed to Tangled. It is the second most expensive film ever made and most expensive animated film ever made with a budget of $260 million. Tangled is also notable for being the third-highest grossing animated film in the Disney animated canon (unadjusted for inflation), behind The Lion King ($783,841,776) and Aladdin ($504,050,219).<br /><br />Overview:<br /><br />Rapunzel (voiced by Mandy Moore) is just about to turn 18 years old. She wants a special gift, to go outside of the tower that she has been in for her entire life. Her mother, Mother Gothel (voiced by Donna Murphy) buntly refuses. As an alternative, she asks for more paint (she paints as one of her many past times in the tower). Mother Gothel agrees to go for it, but it's a 3-days journey away.<br /><br />While she is away, Rapunzel manages to strike up a bargain with Flynn Rider, a thief on the run (voiced by Zachery Levi). At this point her voyage to the outside world begins.<br /><br />Rating:<br /><br />It is quite surprising that this 100 minute animated feature has been given a rating of PG instead of G. In my opinion this may be due to the emotional and psychological content that Mother Gothel generates by her deceptive and manipulative relationship with Rapunzel. (since there is no nudity or offensive language in this film)<br /><br />Plot:<br /><br />Gothel observes a single drop of pure sunlight hit the ground, creating a magical flower with the ability to heal the sick and injured, as well as keep someone young by singing to it. Centuries past as she maintains her youth. Until the pregnant queen of a nearby kingdom gets sick. Her guards manage to retrieve the flower and make a broth out of it. The queen is healed, however, as a side-effect, her daugther is born with hair with the same magical properties.<br /><br />Gothel realises this and kidnaps the child and raises her as her own. But, every year on Rapunzel's birthday, the kingdom sends thousands of floating lanterns into the sky longing for their lost princess to return. Rapunzel desires to find the source of these lights a theit true purpose.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br /><br />This is an excellent family film, with lots of musical content as well as humor. At times I wondered if Dawkins doesn't secretly consider *himself* the pinnacle of the chain of being, since he thinks readers care how he feels about dogs, calculus, his old professors, etc. I just wanted a solid argument of the facts, and what I got was a rough draft written by a middle-schooler with ADD.<br /><br />I also found Dawkins' coining of new words off-putting. \\"Theorum\\" just sounds a bit pweshus--and besides, its pretentiousness is sure to make creationists smirk and evolutionists shake their heads since it still sounds like \\"theory\\" anyhow.
